8.           Cost of different types of interest bearing liabilities.            During 1988,
savings deposits were the most expensive source of funds to ABC. A study of ABC's
transaction costs in Anhui province (under Rural Credit III project) indicated that
while the average cost of raising interest bearing resources was about 6.3?, the
cost of savings deposits was as high as 12.2X. Cost of raising enterprise deposits
ranged from 2Z to 62 depending upon maturity (PBC-prescribed schedule provides low
interest rates for enterprise deposits). Pro-rata overhead costs (comprising
expenditure on staff, fixed assets, and miscellaneous overheads), was at 2.8? in the
case of savings deposits and much lower for enterprise deposits (0.2 to 0.4X).  As
regards resources other than deposits, the cost of PBC loans was about 6.22, 7/
Average cost of interbank borrowings was at 11.2?, and of interbranch borrowings,
9.6Z. Overhead costs on interbank borrowings were 0.8?, and on interbranch
transactions at a surprisingly high rate of 4.3? (possibly because this included the
cost incurred at higher level branches which approve and monitor these loans). The
average cost of longer term resources had been about 7.5? while that of interbank
borrowings around 8.82.
9.           Effect of indexing of interest rates on ABC's cost of funds.  In
September 1988, the government introduced for all banks including ABC, an indexation
of household deposits for three years and above, mainly to discourage increasing
4/ Excludes Income on agency and Interbank business.
S/ Average cost of interest bearing funds; MOF contributed credit funds aro assumed as interest
f roe.
6/ As a part of restrictive credit policy aimed at reduc ng iiflotion, PBC increased interest rates
on deposits and loans In February 1989, and with the deceleration of the Inflation rate since January
1990, reduced the interest rates on deposits and loans, twice in March/April and August 1990 (Annex 2,
Tables 3 and 4).
7/ PBC's interest rate on short-term (annual) loans given as a refinancing facility or for
liquidity management during 1988 was 10.44X (reduced to 7.92% In August 1990). However, PBC's older
loans (with no fixed maturity), which are basically funding loans disbursed from 1979 onwards carry
interest rates of 6.481-7.56 and are used In financing procuremont of agrieultural staple crops for
China's urban food supply. These funds are not usod for financing investments In longer-term assets and
are part of a broad national policy of subsidizing food supplies for 200 million urban dwellers. They
have no impact on resource allocation by ABC or Its competitiveness vis-a-vis other intermediaries, since
credit ceilings and not Interest rates remain China's most important monetary policy Instruments.



63
disintermediation of savings deposits which was contributed by inflationary
expectations. The inflation-indexed deposits carry a 'subsidy' in addition to the
quoted interest rates. The subsidy ensures that the real interest rate on the
indexed deposits at maturity is no less than zero. 8/ Currently, ABC is authorized
to charge interest on loans financed by indexed deposits from households at rates
higher than the normal prescribed rates to cover the additional cost of such
deposits.
10.          Operating costs.   ABC's operating cost excluding interest paid,
increased from 0.95? of total assets in 1986, to 1.22 in 1987 and 1.4Z in 1988, as
against the average interest spread of about 3%. ABC's administrative expenses
(including salaries) are relatively small and increased from 0.552 of total assets
in 1986, to 0.6? in 1987 and 0.752 in 1988 mainly due to expansion of staff and the
branch network and general price increases (Annex 2, Table 6 and explanatory notes).
ABC's "Other expenditure' includes payment of business tax at 5Z of the gross
income, provisions for welfare and development funds, pension funds, and starting
from 1988 provision for bad debts. Excluding provisions (such as development fund
and provision for bad debts in 1988), ABC's "other expenditure" as a proportion of
total assets, increased from 0.42 in 1986, to 0.59% in 1987, and to 0.65% in 1988,
the increase was mainly on account of payment of business tax and the cost of staff
welfare programs including payment of bonuses under the newly introduced contract
system (para. 16 below).
11.          Net Profit and Profit Transfers to Equity.  ABC's net "profit" (or
surplus of income over expenses) after payment of business tax amounted to Y 3.581
billion in 1986 (1.5Z of total assets), Y 4.322 billion in 1987 (1.56% of total
assets) and Y 5.6 billion in 1988 (1.75 Z of total assets) in 1988. Excluding
provisions for bonus, the net profit as a percentage of total assets was 1.36% in
1986, 1.462 in 1987 and 1.56 X in 1988. Thus, in spite of increases in operational
costs, ABC's net profit generally improved. ABC paid income-tax (55Z) and
adjustment tax (7?) to MOF amounting to Y 1.737 billion in 1986, Y 2.315 in 1987 and
Y2.554 billion in 1988.  The balance of profit was transferred to the Credit Fund
(equity), which increased from Y23.62 billion in 1986 (9.83% of total assets), to
Y24.83 billion in 1987 (8.94? of total assets) and to Y26.01 (8.15% of total
assets). Thus, during the past three years, although the absolute amount of equity
increased, its proportion to total assets declined by 1.682. 9/
12.          Issues in ABC's financial Management.  Based on the above analysis, the
following aspects of ABC operations are reviewed below: quality of ABC's loan
portfolio (credit risks), arrangements for creating provision for/writing off of bad
debts, implications of bad debts to ABC's liquidity and profitability (interest
spreads) and adequacy of credit funds (equity), and options available for
8 / Indexing of deposits is only partial, measured by the change In the general price index,
between the quarter prior to the date the deposit is made and its maturity. Further, use of simple
interest rate (no compounding) by Chinese banks, does not guarantee that purchasing power of savings
deposits would be maintained.
9/ During CY1989, ABC's net surplus (before income and adjustment taxes and an approximate provision
for bonus at Y300 million), was lower at about Y3.7 billion or about 0.97% of total assets as against
1.761 of total assets In 1988 mainly because of higher cost of depooits and borrowings. The balance of
profit transferred to the Credit Fund (equity) was also lower at Y1.2 billion (as against Y1.6 billion in
1988), raising the Credit Fund to Y26.98 billion or 7% of the total assets (Annex 2, Tables 6 and 6).



64
replenishment of the Credit Fund.
13.           ABC's Recent Loan Portfolio Review.  The main objective of ABC's recent
nationwide review of the loan portfolio was to begin the process of cleaning up of
its balance sheet by segregating nonperforming assets, evolving specific measures to
collect or restructure or write off nonperforming assets, and upgrade overall
financial management and profitability. The review sought to establish an authentic
data base on the quality of the loan portfolio as of December 31, 1988 indicating,
by sub-branch and purpose, the amounts of loans in arrears, sticky loans and bad
loans. 10/
14.          The portfolio review focussed on speeding up collection of overdue loans
and interest through legal actions, sale of mortgaged properties, restructuring of
enterprises, and by requiring sponsoring agencies to honor their loan guarantees.
The review process required the branches to update financial appraisal of all
borrowing enterprises and, if justified, reschedule loans outstanding against them.
In the case of current loans where credit risks were high, ABC branches asked for
further collateral or financial guarantees from the sponsoring agencies. In
instituting the portfolio review, ABC management also aimed at upgrading decision-
making processes and accountability in loan approvals and collection, and introduce
a portfolio management information system, criteria for credit rating of
subborrowers and guidelines for ranking of branches by their subloan collection
performance.
15.          The portfolio review involved all branches with supervisory
responsibility given to prefecture and provincial branches. Some 100,000 staff was
deployed for this review and every loan outstanding as on December 31, 1988 was
reviewed. To avoid any temporary adjustment of arrear loans using RCC funds or
loans from ABC's subsidiary Trust and Investment Corporations, ABC concurrently
launched a review of RCCs' loan portfolio as well. This enormous task was completed
with professionalism, speed and efficiency. At all levels, ABC established special
Loan Portfolio Review Offices with responsibility to ensure that the review was
carried out objectively and within the time schedule. To ensure that the branches
remain accountable for the Review decisions, ABC HQ informed them that if any
irregular loan is not classified as such at the time of review, responsibility for
any subsequent classification of that loan as sticky or bad, would have implications
to the eligibility of concerned staff for future bonus, and of the branches with
regard to the amount of profit to be retained by them.
10/ By ABC definition, sticky loans are generally those which are in arrears for principal and
Interest for more than two years and include loans to erstwhile brigades and communes where borrowers
have been Identified but repayments are not forthcoming; loans to enterprises which are closed or have
stopped production (for reasons such as lack of market, raw materials or working capital, or due to
prevailing government policy, or bad management); loans which have been used for unauthorized purposes,
loans to households or enterprises on the brink of bankruptcy, and loans disbursed by Supply and
Marketing Cooperatives (for production of specific commodities) at the behest of the government, for
which MOF has asked ABC not to collect principal and interest. ABC reported that on 80X of the loans in
this category, it still receives interest. Bad loans include loans for which the borrower could not be
identified, or borrowers were completely bankrupt, or loans were affected by natural disasters and must
be written off.



65
16.         ABC provided financial incentives to the branches and staff to
accomplish the massive task of portfolio review efficiently and on time, and to
borrowers for cooperating with the review process. Incentives provided to the
branches, staff and borrowers, included the following. The branches could retain
the resources generated by collection of agricultural loans to erstwhile commune
members for new lending; waive interest on loans disbursed to collectives prior to
the introduction of the household responsibility system (1979) and to TVEs
established before the beginning of enterprise reforms, i.e., prior to 1984, if such
enterprises repay the loan; waive penalty interest to enterprises which went
bankrupt during 1985-87, if the restructured enterprises regularly amortize the
outstanding loans as per new repayment terms; use RCCs on a commission basis to
collect loans and allow RCCs to use the amount collected for 2-3 years for their own
lending; use farmer cooperatives and marketing stations for loan collections on a
commission basis; and pay commission to staff on the amount of interest collected on
loans disbursed prior to 1979.
17.          Results of the Portfolio Review.   Based on the results of ABC's
portfoliu review and discussions with different branches during appraisal, the Bank
Group mission came to a view that, by and large, about 20% of ABC's outstanding
loans are overdue (i.e. outstanding beyond the due date); and that out of overdue
loans, about 3Z of total outstanding loans are "bad" i.e. irrecoverable and must be
written off; about 7-8% of ABC's total loans are sticky, i.e., difficult to recover
for various reasons (for definition, see footnote 10); and the balance of about 8Z
of total loans are in arrears which would require rescheduling with corresponding
restructuring or rehabilitation of enterprises.
18.         ABC reported that on about 60-80Z of the sticky loans, ABC still
receives interest and that not all such loans were likely to go bad. ABC branches
are taking several measures to make sticky loans active including legal actions,
restructuring and merger of borrowing enterprises, rescheduling based on updated
financial appraisal of the activities financed, securing additional collateral for
loans, and providing incentives to branches and staff to collect loans, and to
borrowers to repay loans to make these loans active. At this point, it is,
therefore, difficult to estimate what proportion of the sticky loans would finally
go bad. However, at appraisal, the Bank Group mission estimated that about 30-402
of the sticky loans (i.e about 2-3% of the total loans) may eventually go "badf, and
with 3% of the loans already identified as bad, the cumulative amount of bad debts
(including the loans which ABC took over from the People's Bank in 1979) could go up
to 5Z-61 of the outstanding loans as of December 31, 1988 (Y263.16 billion) i.e., at
about Y 13 billion. ll/
19.         As regards the chances of "arrear" loans going bad, since the
classification of loans as bad, sticky and arrear was done only recently and that
the arrear loans which were likely to go sticky or bad have already been included in
II/  At negotiations, ABC provided final data on bad debts by which the bad and sticky loans as of
December 31, 1988 were at 2.43X (Y6.4 billion) and 7% (Y18.4 billion) of he total loans at Y263.16
billion. Assuming that 40% of sticky loans could go bad, the total bad ,-bts as of December 31, 1989 are
estimated at Y13.8 billion or about 6% of total loans. ABC indicated that as of December 31, 1989, its
bad debts were at about 2.18% of outstanding loans (Y379.1 billion) or Y6.6 billion and sticky loans at
7% or Y21.3 billion. By ABC's assessment, the figure of bad debts (including a portion of sticky loans)
as of December 81, 1989 worked out to about Y11 billion.



66
respective categories, it could be assumed (and this was confirmed by ABC) that
loans in "arrear" category could be collected in full.
20.         ABC's recent subloan collection performance.  ABC's annual loan
collections, as a percentage of gross loan disbursements (which increased from Y329
billion in 1986 to 509 billion in 1988) were 90.5? in 1986, 92.2? in 1987 and 94.3Z
in 1988 (Annex 2, Table 7). For commercial loans which accounted for about 79X of
ABC's aggregate annual gross disbursements, or Y320 billion (of which 99? are on a
short-term basis), the collection rate was about 95-96X.
21.         Subloan collections are relatively low in the case of collective
agricultural enterprises (79Z). For township and village enterprises and state-owned
enterprises subloan collection range around 90X of gross disbursements. Even after
allowing for the fact that term loans (about 10? of the total) are repayable in
installments, it is clear that ABC's outstanding loans include a sizeable proportion
of overdue loans, estimated at about 20? of outstanding loans as of December 1988.
Subloan collections for all the three categories should be substantially improved.
22.         In the case of loans to industrial enterprises, loans for acquisition of
fixed assets, poverty alleviation loans, development loans and special loans for
infrastructure, which enjoy repayment periods of 5 to 7 years, the annual
collections have ranged: 36X of gross disbursements for industry (12.4Z of
outstanding loans plus new disbursements); 7.4X for poverty loans which are fully
funded by the People's Bank, 49.21 for development loans and 65? for special loans.
For loans in these categories, ABC must apply improved loan appraisal, supervision
and collection strategies. These aspects will be addressed through the project's
technical assistance component (Annex 9). ABC should also introduce monitoring of
subloan collections with reference to installments fallen due rather than
outstanding loans.
23.         ABC's general experience in regard to the collection of one year working
capital loans is that arrear loans (usually about 102 of annual gross disbursements)
are partly rescheduled and recovered to the extent of 902 in the following 3-4
years. Legal action is needed for recovery of the balance. Eventually about 0.5?
of the gross disbursements go bad. As regards development loans (longer term
loans), which account for about 10? of the annual gross disbursements, the incidence
of bad debts is higher (statistical indicator not available). ABC indicated that,
by and large, annual accumulation of bad debts works out to 1? of outstanding loans
at the end of each year. This partly explains why cumulative bad debts (i.e. bad
debts against loans which ABC took over from PBC in 1979 as well as those against
subloans disbursed after 1979) are now about 5Z-6? of the total outstanding loans.
24.         Existing arrangements for writing off of bad debts.  Until recently,
China's banks including ABC did not have a system of collecting data on arrear loans
with reference to due dates for loan repayments, and separately for principal and
interest due and collected. Also, the banks did not have a system for collecting
information on age-wise classification of outstanding loans. ABC's recent portfolio



67
review substantially corrected this deficiency. 12/ ABC is also introducing a loan
portfolio management system to update semi-annually the information on loan
collection performance. Rescheduling of loans was common in ABC for reasons such as
natural disasters, but in the absence of well-defined criteria, rescheduling
decisions tended to be ad hoc, not necessarily assuring sufficient relief to
subborrowers and rehabilitation of the projects financed. Local governments
provided subsidies to affected subborrowers, but also on an ad hoc basis subject to
availability of funds. Bad debts had been carried in ABC books for unduly long
periods contributing to high overheads, except that about Y2 billion disbursed prior
to 1979 for farm machinery were written off during 1984 and 1985. Until 1988, ABC
profits were fully transferred to the Ministry of Finance (MOF) and no provision was
made for writing off bad debts. Recent government guideline (which became effective
from 1988) provides that specialized banks (including ABC) could make provision for
bad and doubtful debts at the rate of 0.1-0.21 of outstanding loans which seems
inadequate considering the amount of outstanding bad and sticky loans carried by the
specialized banks. In 1988, ABC provided, for the first time, Y300 million for bad
debts against estimated outstanding bad debts of Y13 billion.
Portfolio Review Findings : Issues in and Implications to ABC's Credit
Risks and Financial Viability.
25.         Financing of Bad debts.  Currently, ABC's main sources of financing
nonperforming loans are: Credit Fund or equity contributed by MOF plus annual profit
transfers to Credit Fund, which cumulatively amounted to Y26 billion as at the end
of 1988. If the entire amount of bad debts (at Y13 billion) is notionally written
off from the Credit Fund (equity), the Credit Fund would be reduced by about half to
Y13 billion and ABC's total own funds (i.e. the Credit Fund, retained profits and
the fund for fixed assets) from Y31.11 billion to Y18.11 billion. Consequently, the
proportion of owned funds to total outside liabilities (Y291 billion) will decline
from 11% to about 7?, and to total assets from 102 to 6X. Also, the proportion of
owned funds to loans (risk assets) would be reduced from 12? to about 7Z reducing
the cushion for future bad debts. By international standards, equity of 7? would
not be considered low for a deposit-taking institution like ABC, if interest spreads
are adequate to cover operational costs, make sufficient provisions for bad debts
and pay return on equity at the opportunity cost of capital. The government and ABC
must, therefore, consider instituting arrangements for increasing the provisions for
bad debts and recapitalization of ABC.
26.         PBC's Long-term loans to ABC.  It could be interpreted that ABC is
financing its entire nonperforming loans by long-term borrowings from PBC. PBC's
long-term loans as of December 31, 1988 amounted to Y74.71 billion. These loans
which were disbursed over the past decade (including the amount earmarked as "loans"
when ABC was separated from PBC in 1979) do not have any fixed maturities. Several
loans which have now gone bad were disbursed by ABC at the behest of the government
and were funded by PBC loans. Clearly, ABC would not be able to repay PBC loans
unless corresponding subloans are recovered, without further sacrificing its
12/ Previous IDA-assisted Rural Credit projects required ABC to colloect data on arrears for
subloans disbursed under the projects pending Introduction of a system-wide MIS. As noted, one of the
six In-house studies under Rural Credit III project (i.e. loan collection and write-offs) was
specifically designed to review ABC's loan recovery procedures and performance and the management
Information system nooded.



68
profitability. If ABC's balance sheet is to be cleaned up, the government has two
options: (a) MOF should admit writing off of bad debts from the Credit Funds
(equity), or (b) PBC should write down its outstanding loans with ABC. Option (b)
needs further study and review by the government and ABC, especially to ascertain
what proportion of ABC's loan arrears represent government policy loans for which
PBC has provided budgetary funds, for which writing down of PBC loans seems to be
the only option.
27.         ABC's liquidity and profitability: Constraints to recapitalization of
ABC. ABC has taken the first important step of completing a comprehensive review of
its portfolio. The next step for ABC is to present the findings of the portfolio
review to MOF, PBC (and later to the State Council) and work out ways and timing for
writing off of bad debts. Although ABC's deposits are implicitly guaranteed by the
government as to the payment of principal and interest, accumulating bad debts in
ABC books could eventually lead to a charge on the budget through reduced ABC tax
remittances to MOF or additional equity contributions to ABC. The government must,
therefore, articulate and introduce strategies for recapitalization of ABC,
including providing increased interest spreads through flexible interest rate
policies, provisioning for bad debts, and allowing ABC to retain a larger proportion
of annual profits for business development. Assurances in this behalf were obtained
from the ABC and the government at negotiations (SAR, para. 5.23).
28.         Meanwhile, ABC on its part proposes to introduce several measures to
upgrade its financial management using the proposed project's technical assistance
component (Annex 9). These measures include the following:
-- Currently, ABC lending takes place within the framework of annual
credit plans and ceilings approved by PBC and the government. However,
increasingly, ABC branches will undertake longer range business planning with
greater sectoral, regional and technical assessment backed by financial forecasting;
-- Recent studies have shown that, within the annual credit plans, ABC
branches enjoy considerable autonomy to approve individual subloans. This autonomy
will be further strengthened by asking the branches to approve subloans on well-
defined criteria of credit-worthiness (including enterprise appraisal), technical
feasibility, and profitability (Annex 11);
-- Following up on the loan portfolio review, ABC will introduce a
comprehensive monitoring system with effect from January 1990 which will require the
branches to report quarterly and semi-annually the portfolio condition indicating
regular loans, arrear loans, sticky loans, bad debts and actions taken to collect
overdue loans and interest;
-- ABC would introduce credit-rating criteria for all subborrowers
(especially enterprises) which will determine their eligibility for loans from ABC;
-- ABC has already introduced a contract system at all levels since
January 1988 which has revitalized its operations. Under the contract system, ABC
management agrees with MOF on a number of targets including the amount of income tax
to be paid. Profits in excess of the targeted amount are retained up to 90Z within
ABC system for business development and rewards to staff. The contract system will
be broadened to include more financial indicators, and focussing efforts on



69
upgrading the quality of the loan portfolio including collection of past due loans;
-- ABC has recently placed a strong emphasis on upgrading its internal
audit system which needs further strengthening in terms of staff deployment,
training and coverage which process will be strengthened during the coming years;
-- ABC will upgrade banking skills of its middle and senior level staff
in sectoral and technical appraisal of investments proposed for financing, loan
portfolio management, cost accounting and financial control, and assets and
liabilities management. Staff training and development would focus on these aspects;
-- ABC will review its management information system (including
computerization plans) through the proposed project.

14/ Special loans are disbursed from funds raised by issue of bonds, which carry interest rates of over 70% higher than the normal
prescribed rates, and are provided for Investments yielding high returms

17/ ABC's borrowings from the People's Bank with maturity of lss than one year amounted to Y24.87 billon {including temporary
liquidity management loans and seasonal loans for three months) and long term borrowings (wh,;h were accumulated since 1979
and have no fixed repayment periods) amounted to Y74.71 blillon. Interest rates on PBC loans range from 9% p.a on temporary
borrowings (20 days), 9.72% on seasonal borfowings (three months), and 10.08% on longer term borrowings. PBC refunds Interest
at the rate of 2 1% to ABC on longer term loans, so the effective Interest rate on longer tern borrowings about 8% p.a. The share
of time deposits In the total deposits increased from 16.3% In 1986 to 33.6% In June 1988 and that In Individual (household)
deposits from 78.7% to 79.7%. Deposits from indIviduals for three years and above (which were indexed In September 1988)
amounted to Y 16.48 biMon In 1988 (9.6 % of the total deposits) and Increased to Y 30.9 biMon ( 18% of total deposits) In June
1989.
18/ Direct interbank borrowings amounted to Y4.78 biMon as at the end of December 1988, and the balance represented credit
balances In Interbank transactons/traster of funds.
19/ Credit fund represents the amount originally deposied by MOF with ABC as the working fund (equity) without Interest or dhndend
obligation. The balance of the annual profts (after remXitance to MOF of Income and other taxes) Is transferred In following year
to the Credit Fund.  For accounting purposes, ABC has allocated Credit Funds to offices at four levels Including headquarters,
provincial, prefecture and county level branches.
20/ Net profit represents the balance of profit retained by ABC with th approval of MOF, for transfer to the Credit fund. The net
profit excludes the amount transferred from the annual profis to development and welfare funds, see notes 10-12 to Annex 2, Table
6.
21/ Funds for fied assets represents contra entry for investments In fixed assets (net of depreciation).
22 Others Include liabilities In respect of bonds Issued by ABC, amounts due to subsidiary Trust and Investrnent Corporations,
amouiniheld against pay orders/drafts  sued to clients, net credit on clearlng transactons and transfer of funds among ABC
branches (as of December 31, 1988 debits amounted to Y147.02 blilon and credits Y149.55 billion) and provision for bad and
doubtful debts (Y304 milion). This Item also Includes funds provided by PBC for special purposes Including loans for poverty
alleiation, development of minority areas, rural development In aras noifed by the govemment, and for purchase of foreign
exchange for ABC recently started foreign exchange business.
23/ ABC Is requied to redeposit with PBC, 13% of is total deposits as liquidity reserve.
24/ Deposits with PBC represent ABC HQ's and branches curent accourt baiances used for day-to-day banking and clearing
transactons.
25/ Represent Interbank lending and trasactions between ABC and other banks.

housing and dormitories, buildings for training schools, office equipment Including computers, vehicls, etc.

borrowings from PBC, payments on pay orders, money orders, Interest payments on transactions wIthIn ABC

and retired empboyee (such as tor heating homes and dorms, grain purchases, reglonal hardship), medical expenses for staff
and retired empoyees, cost of common dining faclites for staff, expenses of the workers unlons, and miscellaneous expenses

vehi  tax, stamp fees, cash shortages, and provisions for pension fund, weHfare and devebpment funds, and starting from 1988,
for bad and doubtful debts.
10/ 11/ and 12/ Profit DIstribution. Annual profits after payment of busness tax (5% of gross Income) are distributed as folows
- ABC transfers 21% of the total base  profit (i.e. the  target agreed with MOF under the contract system) to
welfare (essentially bonus) and business development funds which are Included In "other expenditure', Item 9 above. Addionally,
starting fom 1988, ABC Is authorized to retain 90% of the profit made In excess of the target profit for staff welfre and
business development.
- Of the balance 55% Is paid to MOF as Income tax and 7% as adjustment tax;
- The balance 38% is retained In ABC as net surplus for transfer to Credit Fund.

Annex 5
CHINA
FOURTH RURAL CREDIT PROJECT
Planting and Rehabilitation of Apple Orchards
Technical Guidelines for Subproject Implementation
1.          Background.  Apple planting and rehabilitation under the proposed
project would take place in Shaanxi and Liaoning provinces and Beijing municipality,
which together account for about 252 of total apple area and 21% of total apple
production in China. Many households in the project area are traditional apple
growers. However, by international standards, productivity of apple orchards is
low, ranging between 5 and 10 tons per ha.
2.          Reasons for low productivity.  A number of factors have contributed to
currently poor performance of apple orchards, which include the following: many
trees have become old and must be replaced; existing orchards are planted with
predominantly traditional, low yielding varieties (mainly local Kuo Guong); a mix of
varieties and bee-keeping necessary for effective pollination and in turn important
for improved yields and quality of fruits, are generally absent; wide spacings
between apple trees, which are provided by farmers to allow intercropping, reduce
orchard productivity as intercrops tend to compete with apple trees for moisture and
nutrients; insufficient supply of virus-free rootstocks and grafting material has
constrained upgrading and planting of apple orchards; unbalanced fertilizer
application by farmers, especially excessive use of nitrogen leads to vegetative
growth instead of fruits; farmers rarely undertake soil and leaf analysis to
determine optimal fertilizer needs of orchards; and inadequate application of plant
protection chemicals, especially in remote areas has affected yields. Essentially,
extension services to farmers are generally poor which has slowed down the transfer
of know-how from research stations and experimental farms to farmers.
3.          Nursery development.  Although a number of new varieties including Red
Fuji (from Japan) and Starkrimson Red Delicious (from USA) have been imported in
recent years and become popular in China, nursery development is still lagging far
behind in terms of commercial production capabilities for virus-free rootstocks and
grafting material. Consequently, orchard owners produce planting material on their
own farms where testing of rootstocks, buds and scions for virus-free status is
almost nonexistent.
4.            Technical standards for New Investments in Apple Orchards.  Through
the proposed project, ABC and local governments would focus on the prevailing
constraints to establishing modern apple orchards and begin to take specific steps
in support of systematic apple development programs in their respective areas, to
start with for implementing the lending program to be financed by the proposed
project. In approving individual subloans, ABC's project offices and local
Agriculture Bureaus (represented on the Project Management Committees) will pay



90
special attention to the following aspects and ensure that subborrowers comply with
these requirements through subproject supervision.
(a)         Planting Material.  ABC and local technical bureaus will ensure that
only those nurseries which are capable of supplying virus-free rootstocks and
grafting material to subborrowers are selected under the project. ABC will make
arcangements with local research institutions to verify that the selected nurseries
have established necessary facilities to check the quality of the planting material
produced at the nursery.
(b)         Variety selection.  High yielding varieties suited to local conditions
(mainly Red Fuji and Red Delicious) would be introduced under the project with an
optimal number of pollenizer trees planted at regular distance (if possible, in
combination with bee-keeping) to improve effectiveness of pollination so essential
for upgrading both yields and quality of fruits.
(c)         Orchard Management.  Appropriate pruning and tree training practices
will be introduced with due regard to genetic potential of new varieties and
physiological balance of trees through improved extension services and farmer
training.
(d)         Intercropping. Intercropping will annual crops be restricted to first
four years of plantation only.
(e)         Pesticides.  An integrated pesticide application program to protect bees
and predatory insects will be introduced to limit the pesticide application costs
and protect environment. EPA clearance for pesticide packages that will be used on
project-assisted orchards will be necessary.
(f)         Fruit Preservation.  Pre-harvesting measures (including spraying of
fruit protection chemicals) will be introduced to extend the storage period
appropriate to the genetic condition of the varieties and the types of storages
used.
(g)         Irrigation.  Irrigation facilities will be introduced in areas where
monthly distribution of rainfall is not conducive to reach potential fruit
production. However, since irrigation needs of apple orchards are generally limited
to 2-3 waterings per year, the investment costs of irrigation systems tend to be
relatively high. Though the incremental production of apples made possible by
assured irrigation, is usually adequate to provide satisfactory ratep of return on
the investment and a reasonable pay back period, ABC will require subborrowers to
explore how the use of the proposed irrigation systems could be maximized (possibly
by planting other types of fruits or providing irrigation to seasonal and high value
crops). ABC will also require subborrowers to evaluate before the investment-
decision is made, the costs and benefits of alternate irrigation systems (including
open channel, hand-held hose, sprinkler, and drip irrigation) to ensure that the
system chosen for installation is cost-effective and appropriate in terms of water
availability and water requirements. Chemical quality of water and reliability of
the sources of water will be checked in all cases.
(h)         Improvement of Existing Orchards.  Rehabilitation of apple orchards will
generally fall into three broad categories: (a) upgrading of young orchards with



91
trees within the age group of 10-15 years; (b) phased renewal of apple trees within
the age group of 15-25 years; and (c) replanting o. orchards with trees older than
25 years. In category (a), up-grading of low yielding younger traes would involve
mainly an improvement in production techniques (e.g. pruning and tree-training
measures) which in most situations can be done by farmers from their own resources
and would not require any loan assistance from ABC. Only in rare cases where
orchard sizes are large, farmers would need supplementary resources by way of a loan
from ABC. The phased renewal of apple orchards contemplated in category (b)
involves certain risks in that the remaining older trees can hinder the growth of
young trees and such mixed orchards can become breeding places of viruses infecting
the young trees. This option will, therefore, require careful technical evaluation
and completion of renewal in a relatively short period. Uprooting of old trees with
uneconomic production levels (item c) will be supported by the project to take
advantage of increased production possibilities by planting of high value and high
yielding varieties.
(i)         Storage and Transport facilities.  ABC will ensure that the orchards
financed have an access to roads, transport and storage.
5.          General.  In view of the special characteristics of apple planting and
rehabilitation, ABC will finance investments in orchards on the basis of a case-by-
case appraisal which will be reviewed by technical staff at the provincial level,
supported by supervision by the Technical Expert Group at the Head Office.

Annex 6
CHINA
FOURTH RURAL CREDIT PROJECT
Citrus Development in Jiangxi and Guangdong
Technical Guidelines
A. SUMMER ORANGE DEVELOPMENT IN JIANGXI PROVINCE.
1.          General.   Provincial ABC and the Citrus Development Center (CDC) at
Ganzhou will ensure that the project funds are used mainly for planting of improved
varieties of summer (Valencia) oranges. In addition, budwood mother trees should be
healthy, disease free and with desirable horticultural qualities.
2.          Scion sources.  There are some thirty-two scion varieties in Ganzhou
prefecture and selection will be made on the basis of the best available
horticultural information. At this stage, this would include Valencia and Pengan
Navel. CDC will collect variety trial data from all sources in the prefecture and
evaluate it to assess horticultural potential of tested varieties.
3.          Rootstocks.  All rootstocks and scion material will be virus indexed and
from virus free sources. CDC will ensure (and keep records to that effect) that all
planting material, both root stocks and scion, are from controlled mother plantings
of virus indexed material. The problem of yellow shoot prevalent in southern China,
should receive particular attention.
4.          Nursery Requirements and Quality.  Nurseries will be required to provide
trees with the bud union at least 10-15 cms above ground level after soil
subsidence. Nursery technicians will be provided training in field propagation from
seed collection and storage. If necessary, planting of budlings will be deferred by
one year to attain adequate tree size.
5.          Land Suitability and Planting Distance.  Horticultural lime or dolomite
limestone will be added in the terracing stage (mixed with manure and green manure)
to raise pH levels to the 6-6.5 range. The planting distance will be 5m. X 2.5 m.
with immediate trees to be removed at about 6 years with the final number of trees
remaining at about 800/ha.
6.          Irrigation.  Hand held hose system will be used for providing irrigation
to citrus. CDC will also evaluate the macro-jet system introduced at the
experimental station in Lingling state farm in Hunan. CDC will implement a research
and advisory program linking irrigation frequency to plant needs.
7.          Cultural Practices.  CDC will introduce, after necessary evaluation,
methods to incorporate organic fertilizers and optimize tree growth. CDC will also
test pruning methods, frequencies and intensities and make recommendations in this



93
respect.
8.          Packing House Facilities.  Packing house facilities will be equipped
witht grading, washing, fungicidal treatment, waxing, sizing facilities; an
additional separate fungicidal dip tank to treat fruit within 24 hours of harvest
which cannot be put through the packing line within that time or which is placed in
storage for up to two weeks during peak harvesting period; and a 3-ton truck for
field fruit collection.
9.          CDC will obtain technical information on post harvest fungicidal
treatments and storage techniques from South China Institute of Botany, China
Academy of Science, Guangzhou. CDC will arrange training courses for packers,
technicians and managers using the video films produced by the South China Institute
of Botany and other extension media.
10.         CDC's Role. CDC's role in project implementation will include the
following functions: develop short-term and long-term plans for orchard development
including orchard design and planting, nursery development, technology and training
aspects, quarantine services including virus indexing, machinery and equipment
supply and provision of integrated services for production, processing, storage,
packaging, transport and marketing using orange growers' cooperative organizations.
CDC management will have citrus growers' participation and CDC will build up the
required managerial skills in its own organization.
B. HONGJIANG (SUMMER) ORANGE DEVELOPMENT IN GUANGDONG PROVINCE.
1.          Cultural Practices.  Recommendations for Jiangxi province orange
development apply to Hongjiang summer orange development. Further evaluation of
Valencia oranges and late strains of Mandarin will be conducted with a view to
extending the citrus season. ABC will approach the provincial government to arrange
for this evaluation through the Guangdong Horticultural Society and Guangdong
Academy of Agricultural Sciences.
2.          Research. The Guangdong Horticultural Society will introduce, in
addition to ongoing research on reducing the size and number of seeds in Hongjiang
orange, a research program for improving the size of the Hongjiang fruit.
3.          Irrigation.  Irrigation would be provided by hand-held hose system.
Sprinkler systems, being too costly, will not be financed from the project funds
until further economic evaluation of the system is undertaken by concerned research
institutes.
C. CONCURRENT EVALUATION
ABC Head Office will arrange an evalution of the citrus development
component in Jiangxi and Guangdong at the end of 1991 and 1992 and forward reports
thereon to Bank Group for comment.

Annex 7
CHINA
FOURTH RURAL CREDIT PROJECT
Green Tea Rehabilitation in Jiangxi
Technical Guidelines
1.    Background.  The tea rehabilitation program will be implemented in ten
counties--six in Shangrao prefecture (4667 ha) and four in Yuchuan prefecture (1200
ha). Shangrao counties will include: Wu Yian, Dexing, Wan Nian, Wu Shan, Yan Shan
and Shangrao and the Yuchuan counties will include Gaoan, Fenching, Changao, and
Chan Zhu. Most counties in Shangrao prefecture produce green tea while those in
Yichuan produce Oolong tea. Besides rehabilitation of tea, the project would
finance expansion and modernization of preliminary processing capacity in 129
factories--114 in Shangrao and 13 in Yuchuan, and final processing capacity in one
factory each in the two prefectures.
2.    Nature of Rehabilitation.  The area to be rehabilitated falls into three
operational types, based on the condition of existing bushes and the nature of
rehabilitation required as follows.
(a)   Pruning (4693 ha).  Relatively high yielding orchards, with not more than 5Z
gaps, which are showin6 signs of decline in productivity will be pruned at a height
of 30-40 cm from the ground level. The soils will be deep-ploughed to a depth of
30-40 cm along with the application of 33.75 tons/ha of organic matter followed by
1.5 tons/ha of urea every year. This would enable the first 30-50 cm of soil to be
made more fertile with a concurrent increase in soil organic matter (from the
present 1-1.8Z to 2.5-3Z) which will increase moisture retention capability of the
soil leading to a vigorous growth of plants. The plant population will be
maintained at 20,000 plants per ha by gap filling as necessary. To achieve adequate
bush spread, a further light cut would be given one year after pruning which will
promote the growth of further lateral branching. By the second year, bushes are
expected to achieve a diameter of 1.2 to 1.3 m and would cover 80-85% of the ground
surface. Harvest of fresh leaf will commence in the second year after pruning.
(b)   Rejuvenation (294 ha). In areas where the variety of tea is of good quality
and the gaps are not more than 20%, rejuvenation of the bushes will be undertaken by
low cutting of bushes at a height of 5-10 cm from ground level. Such an operation
will promote growth of new shoots through root suckers and thus help the bush to
attain a better spread. The soils would be made more fertile by deep ploughing and
fertilizer applications as in (a) above. Gap filling will be undertaken as required
to maintain the population of plants at 20,000 plants per ha.
(c)   Replacement planting (880ha).  Improved varieties of young tea plants will
established in the inter-rows of inferior varieties of old tea, having more than 602
gaps with a view to upgrading the stock. Farmers will be able to harvest some leaf
from old tea till such time as new tea comes into production in year 3. The old



95
plants would thereafter be uprooted in the 4th or 5th year, leaving behind a
completely new stand of high yielding young tea of good quality. Soil improvement
and fertilizer applications will be similar to that for (a) and (b) above.
3.    Tecbnical Guidelines for tea rehabilitation.  Tea gardens to be rehabilitated
by pruning, rejuvenation and replacement planting will conform to the following
technical criteria:
-- The gradient of the land should not be more than l1Z;
-- Effective soil depth must be at least 0.7 meters;
-- The pH of the soil must be between 4.5 and 6.0
-- Organic matter (carbon) content of the soil must be at least 22;
-- Areas selected for replanting should be those which have not less than 602
gaps; for pruning areas, the gaps should not be more than 5Z; and for
rejuvenation areas, the gaps should not be more than 20X. -- For replacement
planting only varieties approved by the state will be used including the
following:
'i) In Wu Yuan county: Clone Shang Mei Zhou or seed Futing;
(ii) In all other counties: above varieties, along with clone Dan
Mien Bai and seed varieties Long Jun 43 and Ju Ken; and
(iii) Any other varieties which may be approved by the state
in future.
-- At least 200 ha (out of 880 ha identified for replacement) will be
planted with the above named clonal varieties, while seed varieties listed
above may be used for the remaining area.
-- In order to obtain the best quality seed, the tea gardens/areas from which
the seed would be collected should not be plucked from spring until
seeds are collected in October. In tea areas earmarked for seed collection,
the first spring application must be a compound (NPK) mixture, and thereafter
at least two applications of phosphate and potash (P,K) will be given before
seeds are collected;
-_ In commercial nurseries, polybags should be used for raising clonal
plants;
4.    Expansion of Preliminary Processing Factories.  Expansion of preliminary
processing capacities will be undertaken mainly to accommodate incremental tea leaf
producion from the proposed project. To avoid establishment of excess capacities,
ABC project offices will require local Tea/Agriculture bureaus and subborrowers to
provide with each subloan application, a map of the relative leaf production zone
(usually a township) showing location of the factories to be expanded, and of other
factories serving the zone; aggregate preliminary processing capacity available in
the zone vis-a-vis current and potential leaf production; additional capacity
needed; and the timing for establishment of the additional capacity. Subborrowers
will be encouraged to use existing surplus capacities in other factories through
contractual arrangements, before undertaking the proposed expansion. The timing for
the expansion of the preliminary processing capacities, currenty scheduled for year
2 and 3 of the project, will be adjusted in light of these zonal plans.
5.    Final Processing. Expansion of final processing factories, one each in



96
Shangrao and Yuchuan will be undertaken in year 2 of the project, based on an
assessment of the progress made in the proposed tea rehabilitation program for year
1.
6.    Environmental Aspects. Preliminary tea processing utilizes fuelvood for about
702 of its current energy requirements. In future, this is expected to be reduced to
502 while coal use will increase to 302 (from 202) and electricity to 202 (from
102). Even with 502 use of fuelwood, the envisaged project production of 8,000 tons
of preliminary processing tea by year 7 would require 37.000 m3 of fuelwood
annually. To preserve ecological balance, the provincial government should ensure
that areas where trees are felled for fuelwood are reafforested in the year of
felling by intensifying the current afforestation programs. ABC should make this as
a covenant of subloan approval.
7.    Organizational Aspects.  Local Tea/Agriculture bureaus will be responsible
for: training of village and township level technicians on the application of
technical guidelines and criteria for selection and rehabilitation of tea gardens;
timely establishment of nurseries to raise the requisite number of plants and
subsequent distribution of these plants to farmers; monitoring compliance with
technical aspects of the projects; and rectifying any problems that are encountered
in the early stages of project implementation. The county governments will set up a
leading group to coordinate the functions of different organizations, and be
responsible for informing the Tea/Agricultural Bureaus about the annual requirements
of planting material; and coordinating procurement of machinery for tea processing
factories (per Bank Group guidelines).
8.    Concurrent Evaluation.  ABC Jiangxi and ABC Head Office will review the
progress in the tea rehabilitation component semi-annually and report their findings
to the Bank Group through progress reports.



97
Annex 8
CHINA
FOURTH RURAL CREDIT PROJECT
PASTURE-BASED LIVESTOCK DEVELOPMENT
IN INNER MONGOLIA, JILIN, SHAANXI AND LIAONING
Area Management Planning (AMP) 1l
1.    Background.  The project would introduce AMP in eight pilot townships in Inner
Mongolia (5), Jilin (1), Liaoning (1) and Shaanxi (1) to train concerned local
government and ABC staff in using AMP methodology and related computer-aided models
for planning grazed ecosystems. With help of AMP, local project offices and
government technical staff will begin to monitor range-land based livestock
development initially for investments financed by the project and later use this
methodology for planning and monitoring future livestock development in other
grassland areas. The project is supporting the pilot program for AMP since it has
the potential to become an important tool in local governments' rangeland
development processes both at micro-and macro levels. Recognizing the utility of
the AMP approach as a critically needed elemernt in grassland management (which a
plethora of existing laws and rules do not necessarily achieve), ABC and the
provincial governments have used UNDP technical assistance ($50,000) and local funds
to hire the Ranching Systems Group at Texas A & M University for providing technical
training to their staff to use AMP.
2.    AMP concept.  AMP primarily draws upon an integrated approach called the
Resource Systems Planning Model (RSPM) developed by the Ranching Systems Group at
Texas A & M University for nationwide distribution in USDA-Soil Conservation Service
field offices. The system allows the user to inventory forage and animal resources,
conduct enterprise bioeconomic optimization analyses, and determine economic
viability of long term investments in land management technologies. Using a
computer-aided model for planning of grazed ecosystems (RSPM), AMP facilitates
processing of comprehensive inventory and evaluation of resource capabilities and
limitations, as a basis for choosing, implementing and evaluating actions required
to meet the objectives of livestock development within the context of sustainable
development. It must be noted that AMP is not a one-time planning exercise: AMP
would be a tool for continuing assessment of the balance between forage and fodder
availablity, and existing and projected livestock inventories.
3.    In a step-wise procedure, AMP helps the project implementation agencies to
indentify objectives and goals of proposed livestock development, assess the present
and potential resources to meet the objectives, define technologically appropriate
development alternatives best suited to available resources, and establish livestock
development alternatives for individual management units (such as a ranch or a
household or a state farm) with reference to available resources at that level. AMP
1/ Details of AMP are available in the project file.



98
would also help to carry out financial and economic analysis of the livestock
development alternatives to assess the costs, and benefits accruing to the basic
management-unit and periodically review the development alternatives on the basis of
additional information becoming available (including during individual growing
seasons) from the basic management unit for livestock development.
4.    Role of AMP in the proposed project.  For the proposed project, the AMP
process will aim to establish, initially in eight pilot areas, a formal,
monitorable, agreement between villages/townships (sumu) and higher levels of
government that the stocking rates will not exceed those permitted by grassland
resources and that the local governments would take the responsibility to ensure
that these agreements are honored. After necessary training of ABC and local
government staff, this process will be extended in phases to the rest of the project
area. For assessing the grassland resources that will be used by the proposed
project, AMP was not available. The Bank Group therefore required ABC and local
governments to carry out an independent field appraisal for each sub-project area as
a prerequisite for lending under the project.
5.          Rangelands in China and in particular in Inner Mongolia are increasingly
deteriorating due to overgrazing and conversion of highly productive rangeland to
farmlands. In the project areas, rangeland resources are deteriorating in townships
south of Hailar in Hulunbeir league, Zalantun and Zhelimu prefectures in Inner
Mongolia and Baichen prefecture in Jilin. In these areas preliminary assessment of
grassland resources was completed before the appraisal by the Bank Group. In areas
north of Hailar in Hulunbeir league, pasture resources are generally underexploited
and, therefore, project lending will begin there with simultaneous updating of
grassland resource information. For carrying out preliminary assessment of
grassland resources for subprojects in Inner Mongolia, ABC used experts from the
Grassland Research Institute of the Chinese Academy of Agricultural Sciences and for
subprojects in Jilin, experts from the Institute of Grassland Science of
Northeastern Normal University of Changchtn. These experts will be available to ABC
for advice and supervision throughout project implementation and to assist in the
implementation of the AMP pilot program. In Shaanxi and Liaoning where the proposed
rangeland based livestock investments are relatively small, ABC has arranged with
the provincial agricultural bureaus to complete the grassland resource assessment
before lending begins. ABC and the local governments would use AMP to continuously
update this assessment and to provide technical advice to project-assisted
households.
6.    Organizational Arrangements for AMP.  For the purposes of application of AMP
to the proposed project, ABC and the provincial governments would use existing
organizations and staff with coordination provided by local leading groups. At the
grassroots level (township/banner/state farms), a group will be established to
implement the AMP process at individual sub-project sites. Members of this group
will include representatives of ABC, government, Bureau of Animal Husbandry (BAH),
grassland and animal husbandry stations in the project area and, for state farms,
the Bureau of State Farms (BSF). This Group will be responsible to collect
essential rangeland and livestock information for AMP analysis which would be done
by AMP Groups at the county/sumu level. At the county/sumu level, the AMP group
will be the key unit to use a computer-aided AMP model and analyze resource and
livestock inventories, land use plans, forage and fodder budgets and plant-livestock
relationships and provide its assessment and recommendations to the grassroots level



99
AMP Groups. Final decisions concerning increases and decreases in livestock numbers
to ensure that adequate forage and fodder balances exist at the household or
township level will be taken at the township level with assistance from the local
AMP groups. The grassroots AMP groups will monitor the compliance of the household
units with resource conservation advice and livestock development strategies at the
sub-project site generally on a yearly basis. It will also follow-up on the
recommendations of the county and sumu level AMP Group on forage and fodder
development.
7.            The county/sumu level AMP group will include representatives of ABC,
the Bureau of Animal Husbandry (BAH), Butreau of State Farms and representives of the
household management units. AMP groups at the prefecture and provincial levels will
perform essentially planning and monitoring functions for AMP. These higher level
groups will include members of resource and livestock Institutions familiar with
local conditions possessing the expertise to determine if improvement practices
(rangeland, livestock and nutrition) are both economically and ecologically viable.
8.          Follow up.   The provincial governments of Inner Mongolia and Jilin
which have major programs for rangeland-based livestock development recognize the
need to develop an institutional framework capable of mobilizing available expertise
for rational planning of resource use and toward this objective AMP provides a
useful beginning. ABC has signed a memorandum of understanding with the two
provincial governments for the proposed introduction of AMP in six pilot
demonstration sites in the project areas, indicating the role of participating
agencies in implementing AMP and the cost-sharing arrangements. The provincial
governments are also expected to authorise sumu and township governments to collect
a small fee from the beneficiaries of the project to pay for the cost of AMP.
Similar arrangements have been worked out for the pilot projects in Liaoning and
Shaanxi. The Bank Group will assist ABC and provincial governments in the
implementation of the pilot projects through supervision and UNDP technical
assistance.
