Annex 9
CHINA
FOURTH RURAL CREDIT PROJECT
TECHNICAL ASSISTANCE FOR INSTITUTIONAL DEVELOPMENT
OF AGRICULTURAL BANK OF CHINA
1.          Introduction. The main objectives of the project-assisted institutional
development program would be as follows.
-- upgrade ABC's financial management;
-- develop ABC's longer term lending strategies and programs, coinciding with
the national Eighth Five Year Plan;
-- assist ABC in the review of its lending policies for its major client
groups (TVEs, state farms and beneficiaries of poverty alleviation programs);
-- assist ABC to define and upgrade its management information system;
-- provide office technology and equipment to improve ABC's operational
efficiency;
-- provide training to senior staff in financial management, foreign exchange
operations, modern accounting systems and internal audit; and
-- strengthen trainers' training in ABC's three national colleges initiated by
the Third Rural Credit Project, and provide special courses in project
appraisal, management, monitoring and agroindustry financing to senior-and
middle level staff at ABC Headquarters and provincial branches.
2.           Rationale. The overall thrust of the project's technical assistance
program would be to prepare ABC to respond more effectively to its changing role,
from a fiscal organ of the state to that of a full financial intermediary. Since
its inception in 1979, ABC has taken important steps to build up considerable in-
house capability in financial management. Previous IDA-assisted projects have
contributed to this process (SAR, paras. 1.10-1.14). The proposed project would
expand upon this effort and support new initiatives.
3.          Design of the Institutional Development Program.  Rural Credit I and II
projects introduced ABC branches in three provinces (Guangxi, Fujian, and Hunan) to
standard techniques in financial and economic appraisal of investments (including a
project appraisal manual prepared by ABC with IDA assistance) and related staff
training. Rural Credit III Project continued this activity in six other provinces
(Sichuan, Yunnan, Guizhou, Anhui, Henan and Hubei) and also introduced a trainers'
training program in project appraisal and other banking subjects for ABC's three
national training institutes and lower level secondary vocational schools to build
in-house training capabilities in ABC and extend the benefit of training to
provinces not served by specific IDA projects and eventually to the ABC system
nationally. It was only with Rural Credit III Project (which was larger in terms of



101
both geographical coverage and the Credit amount) that ABC and the Bank Group began
to place much emphasis on upgrading ABC's overall financial management.
4.          As a part of Rural Credit III project and the preparation of the
proposed (fourth) project, specific analytical activities focussing on ABC's
financial management issues included: the six system reform studies; a financial
appraisal of ABC's operations in Jiangxi and Liaoning provinces by a consulting
group from France (Annex 2, Attachment 1, para. 1), and ABC's recent nationwide loan
portfolio review (Annex X, Attachment 13). Additionally, the Bank Group's recent
report on China's Financial Sector Review (1989) focussed on issues in institutional
development of China's specialized banks including ABC. Specific initiatives that
would be supported by the proposed project have been derived mainly from the
recommendations of these four major tasks. Given the enormous size of ABC
operations, some of the institutional development initiatives would necessarily be
of a catalyst in nature, requiring much follow-up by ABC headquarters. The Bank
Group would assist ABC in this follow up through project supervisions, possible
follow-up LoanlCredit and by arranging bilateral technical assistance.
5.          Against this background, the main elements of ABC's institutional
development program are reviewed below and summarized in attached Table 1 which also
indicates how various activities would be financed, their timing and ABC departments
responsible for implementation.
Main Elements of ABC's Institutional Development Program
I. FINANCIAL MANAGEMENT
6.          Financial Management Symposium (March 1991) (Annex 9, Table 1, item 1).
A symposium in ABC's financial management will be held in Beijing in March, 1991.
The timing of the negotiations was agreed to during negotiations. ABC presidents,
vice presidents and senior management (directors of planning, credit, funds and
accounts departments) at the headquarters and provincial branches, officials of MOF,
SPC and PBC, overseas experts and Bank Group staff would participate. ABC and other
participants would prepare background papers for the symposium, based on the
findings and recommendations of the six in-house systems reform studies completed
under the Third Rural Credit Project, Sema Group's financial appraisal of ABC
operations in Jiangxi and Liaoning, ABC's loan portfolio review and the main
recommendations of the Bank's Financial Sector Review (FSR) in so far as they relate
to ABC's institutional development and broader financial sector reform aspects. The
symposium would focus on the progress made by ABC so far, in regard to financial and
other reforms and address mainly the following issues.
-- Strategic Planning. Chinese banks including ABC, operate on the basis of
annual business and credit plans. Medium-or long-range planning backed by a
quantified analytical base, and clear linkages with annual planning and budgeting
process is, by and large absent, in all banks including ABC. The symposium would
discuss the ways in which the long range planning processes could be introduced in
ABC.
-- Annual Credit Plan. Substantial improvements could be made in ABC's credit
plan formulation and approval processes focussing on the point that, over the medium
term, Chinese banks including ABC should be using indirect methods (interest rates)



102
to ensure more efficient resource allocation, and that ABC must institutionally
prepare to assume greater responsibility in subloan decision-making, requiring more
rigorous credit rating of clients and risk analysis.
-- Lending Policy. ABC has recently issued elaborate guidelines for credit-
rating of clients, collateral and guarantee requirements, and lending authority of
branches. However, ABC should expand upon these guidelines for more effective loan
management to cover aspects such as credit-deposit ratios, maturity dietribution of
'Loans, concentration of credit to a single borrower or sector, flexible interest
rates which would vary according to credit risk besides loan maturities, variable
down payment requirements consistent with the credit eligibility of clients, etc.
-- Monitoring of Loan Portfolio. Recently, ABC has taken important steps in
evaluating its loan portfolio nationwide (Annex 2, Attachment 1, paras. 13-19) and
by issuing trial regulations for portfolio monitoring and evaluation on a continuing
basis. 1 Future actions on this topic would include: evaluation of the new
monitoring system in terms of its adoption and use by the branches, its integration
with the accounting system, impact on collection of irregular loans, and
contribution to improved portfolio management.
-- Assets and Liabilities management. The structure of ABC's assets and
liabilities is rapidly changing, so also the interest cost of various sources of
funds and risks on different types of credits. ABC's reliance on interbank market
has also increased and its recourse to the People's Bank for liquidity management is
more frequent than before. ABC should regularly monitor the structure of its
liabilities and assets at all levels, interest spreads, risks and the implications
to overall liquidity and profitability of the system (Annex 2, paras. 2-10)
-- Assets Valuation. Chinese accounting principles in China though broadly
consistent with international standards do not cover some important aspects as
follows: accounting of asset losses and bad debts is based on actually realized
amounts and the practice of establishing reserves or writing down of assets does not
exist; the principle of valuation of assets at lower of cost or market value is
still not used; and provisions for uncollectible or bad debts are not established
except that starting from 1988, MOF has authorized ABC to make provisions up to
0.1-0.2Z of the outstanding loans, which amounts, at least for several more years,
would be inadequate to clear the backlog of bad debts (Annex 2, Attachment 1, para.
18).
-- Internal Audit and Controls. During the past two years, ABC has placed
strong emphasis on upgrading internal audit. A separate internal audit department
has been set up at all levels (down to prefecture) and coverage of transactions by
1/  ABC's now monitoring procedure would require all branches to classify loans into regular ;nd
irregular (overdue) loans, and all irregular loans into three categories, namely overdue loans (generatly
less than two years arrears), sticky loans (loans difficult of recovery) and bad or Irrecoverable loans
(Annex 2, attachment 1, footnote 10). ABC's higher level branches (and finally headquarters) would monitor
the status of the loan portfolio on a quarterly basis and ensure that all irregular loans are audited. The
branches' business plans would lay down specific targets and action programs for recovery of ovordue and
sticky loans and for legal actions against borrowers of bad loans. ABC presidents at all levels would
quartorly review the progres and take further steps to Improve loan portfolio management. Staff rewards
and penalties would in future be based, among other things, on the degree of performance in the improvement
of tho quality of loan portfolio.



103
audit has been increased. The number of staff assigned for internal audit was
nearly doubled. However, ABC recognizes that internal audit organization needs to
be further strengthened in terms of additional staff, training of audit staff,
defining audit procedures and ensuring independence in reporting of and follow up on
audit objections. ABC is particularly looking for international experience in this
regard.
-- Income and Expense Statements and Balance Sheets. ABC's annual statements
of income and expenditure and assets and liabilities provided to outside agencies
(including bilateral and multilateral agencies) do not contain adequate details
which are essential for financial analysis. Internal financial reports are not
released except to the government (MOF and PBC) and the format is designed mainly to
monitor compliance with the credit plan and expense control rather than to
facilitate detailed financial analysis. For improved financial management, ABC must
introduce internationally accepted formats for compilation of income and expenditure
statements and balance sheet.
-- Contract System. The contract system introduced in ABC needs to be
broadened to include more financial indicators to increase its effectiveness and
incentives to staff.
-- Financial management in RCC system. ABC has the leadership responsibility
and supervisory role with regard to the vast RCC system. ABC recently asked all
RCCs to evaluate their loan portfolios as of end 1988, on the same lines as was done
in ABC (Annex 2, attachment 1, para. 15) and to follow loan monitoring system which
ABC has introduced for its own branches. The symposium would discuss issues in RCC
system's financial management and the initiatives ABC, PBC and the government should
take to strengthen the RCC system. The current relationship between ABC and the RCC
system is mainly complementary and the issue is, how far i, is, or should be
competitive to promote efficiency of rural financial intermediation? The government
has also agreed to review this aspect through a study of Rural Investment and
Finance, which it would carry out under the proposed project (SAR, paras. 4.26 and
5.11).
7.          The -ymposium is expected to create an awareness among ABC's senior
management nationwide on key issues in ABC's rural financial operations and provide
a forum to discuss further initiatives needed within ABC system, and by PBC and MOF.
Details of symposium's timing, topics, participation and responsibility for
discussion papers were firmed up during negotiations. After the symposium, ABC
Headquarters would hold (at least two) workshops for senior and middle level
management at the provincial level, at different centers during 1990-91. During the
mid-term review of the project scheduled for the second half of CY92, the government
and the Bank Group would review the progress made in introducing new initiatives in
ABC's financial management.
8.          Upgrading senior staff skills in financial management. (Annex 9, Table
1, Item 2). The project would finance at least two ( and up to five) for senior
staff at HQ and provincial branches, in bank management including risk analysis,
financial analysis, accounting systems and cost analysis, and internal audit systems
in Beijing and other provincial centers. ABC would hire experts from abroad for the
initial few workshops.   The project would also provide funds to finance ABC senior
staff visits to overseas banks for exposure and training in financial management.



104
II. FORMULATION OF STRATEGIES
FOR MEDIUM AND LONG TERM LENDING
9.          Formulation of medium and long term lending program and related studies
and reviews including preparation of Rural Financial Sector Project for possible
Bank Group assistance in FY93) (Annex 9, Table 1, item 3). ABC is predominantly a
commercial bank for the rural sector. However, consistent with the government
policy to shift from budgetary (grant) financing to loan financing of commercial
investments by using institutional finance, ABC has begun to increase its medium-and
longer lending for bankable investments in crops, livestock, aquaculture and
agroprocessing. ABC has not yet established policies for providing long-term
finance for several types of investments and institutional arrangements to carry out
sectoral analysis and techno-economic studies. With technical assistance provided
by the proposed project, ABC would undertake several subsectoral studies, prepare
guidelines for subproject appraisal, technical support arrangements, and cost
recovery for investments in different subsectors including small irrigation projects
(with focus on grain, cotton and oilseeds production), livestock, aquaculture,
agroprocessing and investments involving technology transfer ("Spark" and "Flame"
programs). Based on the subsectoral studies and related field work, ABC would
prepare an indicative program for medium and long-term lending within the framework
that would be set by the Eighth Five Year Plan (expected 1991-95). ABC would also
use this work to develop a lending program for likely future lines of credit from
the Bank Group and other multilateral agencies. The project's technical assistance
would finance local costs and provide foreign exchange for structured overseas
visits by ABC's senior staff to financial institutions abroad, to study their
experience in longer term lending including project preparation, techo-economic
appraisal, market analysis and loan management.
III. ABC'S LENDING STRATEGIES FOR MAJOR CLIENT GROUPS
10.         Development of ABC's lending policies for major client groups.  Outside
the procurement finance for grains and other agricultural products which accounts
for over 60Z of ABC's total disbursements annually, ABC's major lending is to
Township and Village enterprises (TVEs) and the State Farm System.  Since 1984, ABC
also started playing an important role in poverty alleviation programs when the
government began to provide to ABC special funds for providing concessional loans to
poverty groups. ABC faces important institutional and financing issues in the
management of loans to the above three categories of clients, which it proposes to
address with technical assistance from the project.
11.         Township and Village Enterprises (Annex 9, Table 1, item 4(i)). During
the past decade, the Chinese economy witnessed a remarkable growth in rural
industrialization and other non-agricultural production accompanied by large
increases in rural personal incomes and major shifts in the structure of the rural
labor force. This growth was characterized by proliferation of township and village
enterprises (TVEs). In recent years, the TVE sector has faced an increasing
competition and market saturation, complicated by economic austerity measures
(including credit ceilings, restrictions on access to raw materials and energy)
which the government had to introduce to curb inflationary pressures. This has
resulted in numerous investment failures and financial problems for the TVE sector
which has led the government to introduce restructuring and rationalization of the



105
sector. ABC and the RCC system are directly involved in this process since together
they have provided a credit of over Y80 billion ($17 billion) to TVEs. ABC alone
has financed over half a million TVEs with outstanding loans of about Y40 billion or
502 of the total. ABC's recent loan portfolio review has indicated that the problem
of bad loans is more serious in the TVE sector than in other areas of lending. 2/
12.         Against this background. the project would provide technical assistance
to ABC to undertake a TVE restructuring study on TOR acceptable to the Bank Group
and define lending strategies addressing diverse issues in TVE financing, focussing
on the following: recent initiatives in restructuring the TVE sector (much of it
through administrative measures rather than market forces) and related financing
packages; ABC's loan collection problems in the wake of numerous investment failures
and TVEs' financial problems; recent developments in the legal and regulatory
framework for TVEs and how these affect ABC operations; institutional skills (staff
training) needed within ABC to efficiently manage the TVE portfolio; arrangements
for technical assistance and support services for TVEs to improve their management;
and policy for financing of new TVEs, especially in the context of uncertainties
currently faced by the sector in regard to its future role in the Chinese economy.
This analysis is critical for ABC to develop an approach to its future financing of
the TVE sector. This review would also be useful for preparation of the likely next
project with ABC for possible Bank Group assistance, which would among other things
focus on rehabilitation of TVEs financed by ABC.
13.         ABC Financing of State Farms. (Annex 9, Table 1, item 4(ii)).  The
project would provide technical assistance to ABC to review its past lending to the
state farm system and develop future strategies. Specifically, this review would
document ABC's experience with lending to different types of state farms and
enterprises started by tnem; the role that the state farm system can play in
modernizing the rural economy through cooperation with the household farms and
enterprises located outside the boundaries of the state farms, the strengths and
weaknesses in the state farms' present organization, management, resource
mobilization and investment strategies, government and ABC role in financing state
farms, and cost recovery aspects of ABC and government financing. The review would
be based on detailed study of ten representative state farms (including those
financed by previous IDA projects with ABC), use these findings to recommend any
reforms needed in ABC financing of state farms, and how ABC could assist in
accelerating the reform processes in the state farm system through its expanding
lending operations. The review would also address the question as to how ABC can
strengthen its own organization and staff skills to process and supervise lending to
the state farm system, which is becoming more complicated and diversified.
2/ The most atriking characteristics of China's TVE sector have been its dynamism (rapid growth,
ability to take advantage of opportunities, and flexible adjustment), competitiveness (vis-a-vis the stat.
cector), divorolty (in terms of Institutional arrangements. level of development, etc), outward orientaton
(in torso of product markets), community orientation (most firm, hove close formal and informal ties with
their home communities, and very few relocate across community boundaries) and factor Immobility (labor,
capital, and ethor key factors of production to a large extent do not move among communities which Is
eonerally the cose for the entire Chinoee economy).



106
14.         The state farm system has emerged as one of the major borrowers of ABC
3/ with the declining share of the government budget in financing investments in
agriculture. This trend is also reflected in the share of the state farms and
state-owned enterprises in IDA-assisted lending programs which has been sizeable.
As a part of project preparation, the Bank Group, therefore, reviewed recent
developments in the state farm system. This review indicated that the state farm
system has undergone fundamental changes during the past decade and, contrary to
common belief, the system no longer operates along the soviet-style models but is
rapidly becoming a diverse system of profit- maximizing entities with expanded roles
for family enterprise at the farm level.
15.         China's state farm system is indeed enormous.  Most of the state farms
were set up in the early years of the People's Republic as a part of the army-led
land reclamation program to strengthen defence of the border areas, resettle
demobilized army units, and promote economic development of the frontier areas. By
the end of 1988, there were 2,124 state farms with a total area of 33.7 million ha
of which cultivated land was about 4.4 million ha, forest land, 2.5 million ha
(including 393,000 ha of rubber plantations), rangeland, 15.3 million ha, and water
surface area of 760,000 ha. Total population of the state farm system is about 11.4
million of which 5.1 million are workers and staff. Prior to 1979, the state farm
system was heavily influenced by central planning processes, with little or no
incentiver to individual households, farms or enterprises within the system. The
state farm system consequently reported enormous losses and was rarely subjected to
economic forces or financial discipline.
16.         Reforms in the past ten years have significantly changed the character
of the state farm system 4l: first, the production responsibility system (PRS) which
proved successful in dismantling communal farms and promoting productivity in
agriculture and rural industry, was extended to households and enterprises within
the state farm; second, the government introduced a financial contracting system by
which the state farms were allowed to retain profits earned in excess of the
contractual obligations to the government. and which also restricted the flow of
government subsidies to the minimum to promote economically efficient management;
third, the state farm system changed the focus of their economic activities from
purely agricultural production to a combination of agriculture, agroprocessing,
light industry and trade; and fourth, state farm-sponsored enterprises were made
"independent economic entities" responsible for their profit and loss. These
changes have revitalized the state farm system, made their operations more
diversified and market-oriented, and increased their role in development of the
rural economy.
17.         Another remarkable feature has been the growing economic interdependence
S8 ABC u annual Ilnding to the stae faorm system Increosed from Y12.7 b111ton In 1986 to Y24.8 billIon
in 198S with outosnding balance. at the end of the year Increasing from Y8.96 billion to Y13.41 billion.
Compared to the stte farms, ABC lending to Individual (specialized) households has been sml ir, increasing
from Y4.68 billion In 1988 to Y7.8 billion in 1986 with outstanding balance as at end of 1988 at Y8.68
bilIIon. The proportion of medium-and long-term loans to total loans extended to the state form system has
increased from 12.6w In 1986 (YI.S1 bilI on to 16M1% in 1966 (Y2.0 billion).
4/ A paper on Recent developments In China's State Form System  is ovaltable on project file.



107
between the state farms and surrounding households and enterprises, with state farms
providing credit, technology, improved seeds, breeding stock, feed, and marketing
and other services to households and buying from them raw materials for state farms'
processing enterprises. Additional employment potential created by this economic
cooperation has been benefitting households both in and outside the state farms.
Increasingly, ABC is also using the state farm system as a channel for credit to
smallholders through contractual arrangements between the state farms and households
outside the boundaries of the state farms. ABC's direct lending to individual
(specialized) households though growing is relatively small firstly, because
provision of non-schematic lending is mainly the function of RCCs and secondly, ABC
is not institutionally designed (with branches down to township level and not in
villages) to serve numerous households, and its loan administration costs for
smallholder lending are, therefore, relatively high. The proposed technical
assistance to ABC for articulating its lending policies for the state farm system,
and defining the scope and strategies for institutional development of the state
farm system is therefore, critical and timely.
18.         Review of ABC's Poverty Alleviation Lending (Annex 9, Table 1, item 4
(iii)). As a part of the proposed project's preparation, the Bank Group reviewed in
detail the role played by ABC in handling poverty alleviation funds channeled by the
government and the People's Bank, and how the Bank Group could assist concerned ABC
departments in upgrading the efficiency of the poverty programs. The proposed
project would provide technical assistance to ABC to introduce standard techniques
of financial and economic appraisal to poverty alleviation lending to upgrade their
effectiveness and cost recovery; develop models which could help realize the
intended objectives of the poverty programs; explore possibilities of supplementing
the poverty alleviation by commercial funds to finance infrastructure investments
(processing, storage, transport); and develop organizational arrangements to provide
technical and support services using different technical bureaus. Through the
project's technical assistance, ABC would use international experience in poverty
alleviation programs, learn from failures of poverty programs in other countries,
and use this exercise to redesign its own programs. The Bank Group would closely
interact with ABC in this review and ABC staff would visit poverty programs in other
developing countries.
19.         Dimensions of rural poverty in China.  Available evidence indicates that
absolute poverty in China declined to very low levels during the last decade.
China's absolute poor number about 70 million or close to 7Z of total population
(1987 data; it is possible that this number is now higher due to inflation during
the last two years and relatively slower growth of the economy and per capita
incomes in certain regions). It is estimated that the number of rural inhabitants
with incomes less than the government's 1986 absolute poverty line of Y200 (US$54)
declined from between 200-220 million in 1979 to about 70 million in 1986. As a
proportion of total rural population, the absolute poor declined from nearly 302 in
1979 to 10% in 1986 (and about 8.2% in 1987). Incidence of absolute and relative
poverty in China's urban areas is extraordinarily low.
20.         Until the mid-1980s, Chinese government did not consider poverty
alleviation as a special development issue, and its assistance to the poor took the
shape of scattered relief operations. Successful introduction of PRS was regarded
as the main route to alleviate widespread rural poverty. However, a study by the
former Research Center for Rural Development (RCRD) indicated that the benefits of



108
PRS have not been even across the country, especially in remote and mountainous
areas and to address this problem, several initiatives other than PRS would be
needed.
21.         In 1986, the government, therefore, formulated a new policy for poverty
alleviation and indicated the main strategies which provided for combining poverty
alleviation programs with long-term regional development programs; providing
incentives for sustained economic development of poorer areas; coordinate use of
poverty alleviation funds from different sources for improved results; emphasize
extension of modern technology to poorer areas through human resource development;
provision of inputs and support services; and increasing use of economic entities
such as specialized households, rural enterprises. service agencies, nongovernmental
organizations in the implementation of the poverty alleviation program. The main
components of the poverty alleviation program include: construction of
infrastructure, development of agriculture and rural industry, human resource
development and policy adjustment. To facilitate the implementation of the poverty
alleviation program, the government redefined poverty lines (ranging between Y150 to
Y300 per capita) and identified 325 counties, mainly in (i) mountainous areas, (ii)
grassland (pastoral and semi-pastoral) areas and (iii) areas characterized by
ecological fragility (Gansu and Ningxia). In addition to the central government
support, several counties classified as poor receive provincial and regional
support. A Poor Area Development Office in Beijing handles day-to-day operations
under directions from a national leading group in Beijing. A training center, and
economic development service center have been set up in Beijing to assist in the
implementation of the program and EDI is likely to support a training program for
key staff during 1990. Implementation of the program is carried out by various
government and nongovernment agencies and the banks.  However, the banking system
plays an important role in the implementation of the program.  Out of annual funding
of Y4 billion, the government budget provides only Y 1 billion and the rest is
provided by the banking system.  ABC manages an annual lending program of Y1.4
billion earmarked for poverty alleviation.
22.         ABC's role in poverty alleviation.  As the poverty alleviation program
is new, ABC's lending policies and procedures for this program are still evolving.
ABC focuses mainly on lending for agriculture, animal husbandry, fishery, household
handicrafts, and small mining industries. A large proportion of loans have been
provided directly to households. Economic results of these program were reportedly
unsatisfactory. Monitoring and evaluation criteria introduced in 1988 are very
limited in scope. Lending procedures involve allocation of funds by ABC
headquarters and provincial branches to counties. Subprojects selected by local
leading groups are approved by ABC within this quota. Interest rates on loans are
charged by ABC at the current rate for one-year loans and to provide ABC a minimum
spread of 2.4Z, MOF provided interest subsidies at 5.5Z in 1988. MOF interest
subsidies are explicit since borrowers are required to pay interest at ABC rates
first and then claim the subsidy from MOF. However, ABC seems to be losing money on
this lending operation since loan and interest collections are reportedly poor.
23.         The proposed project provides an important opportunity for the Bank
Group to assist ABC and the government in upgrading the concepts, design,
implementation arrangements for the poverty alleviation programs and reduce their
financial costs to the government and ABC. ABC and government agencies lack
experience in handling development oriented poverty programs and this is an area



109
where the Bank Group could pass its experience on to China.  At negotiations, ABC
and the government agreed that, by December 1990, detailed TORs for the above three
reviews (paras. 10-22) of ABC's lending strategies for major clients (TVEs, State
Farms and Poverty Groups) would be prepared for the Bank Group's review, indicating
schedule of task description, timing, staffing, report preparation and review
arrangements.
IV.ABC'S MANAGEMENT INFORMATION SYSTEM: LONG TERM STRATEGY
24.         Review of ABC's Management Information System, related computerization
and longer term strategies (Annex 9, Table 1, item 5) ABC's current management
information system (MIS) is in the initial stage of development and used mainly to
monitor compliance with the credit plan, tracking deposit mobilization against
targets, measuring revenues and controlling expenses against budget. 5/ With the
expanding business and changing role of ABC from an administrative agency to a
financial institution, ABC recognizes that its MIS must be reinforced. Since 1985,
ABC has invested Y400 million (about $100 million) on hardware and the recent annual
budgets are close to Y100 million ($30 million). Little money has been spent on
software development and human resource development and a longer term strategy for
MIS design, software development and hardware acquisition has not yet been
considered. Before ABC invests further amounts in hardware, it must take a hard
look at the current status of MIS and develop short- medium- and long-term
strategies. The project would, therefore, provide technical assistance to ABC to
review (i) its management information system in light of international practices and
experience and suggest improvements; (ii) review ABC's ongoing program of
computerization (hardware acquisition and software development) and (iii) develop
technology options and longer term strategy for computerization program. The
expected action plan on MIS development and automation would be broadly as follows:
3- CY1991:        Diagnosis of ABC's existing information system, and training of
ABC staff in Information System in Banking and Information System
methodology;
-- CY1991:        Develop ABC's Master Plan for MIS;
-- CY1992:        Develop regional prototype information systems for deposits and
withdrawals with customer orientation;
-- CY1993-94s     Extend regional prototypes to other regions;
-- CY1993,94,95: Introduce MIS for other business activities;
-- CY1995-2000: Generalize MIS throughout ABC system.
25.         The project would support the first two phases (1990-91 program).  This
approach was evolved with ABC as a part of the financial appraisal by the Bank Group
consultants (the Sema Group) which was discussed with ABC HQ during the project
appraisal. Some modifications in this approach are possible when the work on
5/ A similar situation exists in other specialized banks per FSR (SAR,
paras. 1.17 and 2.4).



110
Phase I is completed.
26.         Computer hardware in China's banking industry.  During the past decade,
development of information processing systems in Chinese banks included the purchase
of mainframes in PBC, BOCOM, ABC and CITIC, all of different models with no
capability to communicate with each other. These computers are used mainly for
processing information at the headquarters level. Simultaneously, the banks have
bought a large number of micro-computers (around 50,000) to equip branches and
business offices, for daily business operations and office automation. A few
offices are linked with "local area networks' to transfer and consolidate data at
local levels.
27.         Hardware at ABC HQ. ABC head office is equipped with an IBM 4381
mainframe, with a storage capability of 16 MB, linked to a disk storage capacity of
6.4 GB, consisting of 8 disk units of 800 MB each. This mainframe is linked to 40
IBM PC/XT (and local compatibles). Four IBM 3422 magnetic tape units, working at
speeds from 1600 to 6250 Bps are connected to this mainframe. The head office is
also equipped with 50 local IBM 5550 micro-computers, with Chinese characters
processing capabilities used mainly for office automation. The application system
used is the IBM system support with Chinese characters utilities on micro-computers,
and languages are COBOL, SQL/DS and CICS (the latter two on mainframe only). The
total cost of the system is US$ 1.5 million. The mainframe is still not in full
operation as the networking and software development are in progress. The system
will be fully used in the near future to store and process most data sent by lower
level branches.
28.         Hardware at ABC branches.  From the county level to the provincial
level, the branches are equipped with micro-computers linked by modems up to HQ.
While a four-level network (county-city-province-headquarters) is predominant, some
provincial branches are equipped with a few higher level computers (AL"OS 3068).
29.         Telecommunications.  From county to provinces and province to
headquarters, telephone networks are used for batch file transfers. Some real time
networks are in the process of development.
30          Coverage of ABC MIS and automation.  ABC has generally automated
accounts consolidation, and recording of daily-deposit receipts and withdrawals
including report printing and automatic passbook updating. ABC has also automated
payroll (salary and bonuses) reports (usually quarterly) at the municipal,
provincial and headquarters level and recording of basic staff information and
computerized household income and expenditure surveys. By and large, ABC's
automation and related MIS are thus accounting oriented; it handles mainly
statistical data processing (at unit level and bottom-up transmission) which saves
considerable time and administrative costs and improves upon the accuracy of the
information. Customer orientation in MIS is still very limited (for individuals or
enterprises) and MIS also does not handle portfolio analysis. Software development
is handled within ABC by its data-processing development institutes at Wuhan and
Shanghai. ABC lacks skilled personnel needed for using advanced technologies and
methods to design and use MIS for which considerable human resource development will
be needed.



ill
V. HUMAN RESOURCE DEVELOPMENT
(TRAINERS' TRAINING AND STAFF TRAINING)
31.        Human Resource Development (Annex 9, Table 1, Items 6 and 7) The main
features of the proposed staff training program would be as follows:
--   continue support for upgrading the faculty of ABC's three national-level
Senior Staff Colleges at Tianjin, Changchun (Jilin) and Wuhan (Hubei) and of ABC's
secondary and vocational training schools in project appraisal, management,
monitoring and evaluation, and agroindustry financing;
--   and organize special courses on a priority basis for project/credit staff
in ABC HQ and project provinces as well as in provinces not so far covered by
IDA/IFAD/ADB projects and provide overseas training to selected staff in project
appraisal, monitoring and evaluation, and agroindustry financing;
32.            Trainers' Training.  ABC's three national-level training institutes
are responsible for the training of ABC's management personnel and of teachers for
ABC's 170 secondary vocational and staff training schools which offer training to
its operational staff. The ongoing Rural Credit III Project provides technical
assistance to ABC for upgrading the faculty and facilities at the three colleges,
which in turn would train the managerial staff, and teachers at lower level schools
in key subjects including project appraisal, market analysis, small enterprise
management, modern banking practices and teaching techniques. The Rural Credit III
Project envisaged that several new short-term courses would be introduced and
existing courses would be upgraded at the three colleges and vocational and staff
training schools. Through improved instruction, it is envisaged that ABC schools
would gradually turn out better trained managerial and operational staff nationwide,
thus helping to improve the range and quality of ABC services, and contributing to
more efficient internal management. Specifically, the Rural Credit III project
provided for: (a) overseas training for selected faculty members of the three
colleges (including senior management staff who often teach at these colleges); and
(b) visits of overseas teachers (consultants) to assist the three colleges in
designing and teaching new courses; and (c) purchase equipment, library resources
and facilities for reproducing teaching materials.
33.            The trainers' training under Rural Credit III project had a slow
start. Proposals developed by British Council, Agricultural Cooperative Development
Institute in USA, USDA's Training Institute, and the Canadian International
Cooperative Training Institute for training of ABC staff in China and abroad were
not accepted by ABC because of its perception that the cost per trainee was too
high. Identification of suitable candidates for overseas training and their
language training also involved considerable delays. ABC has now selected three
teachers, one from each of the three colleges, to participate in the international
course on bank management in Thailand during 1990. On return, the three teachers
would hold similar courses in China. In September 1989, ABC also held a four-month
course to upgrade English language skills of teachers who are likely to be selected



112
for training abroad. 6/ In view of the high cost of overseas training. ABC now
proposes to pay for overseas trainers to visit China to teach at various training
courses for trainers and senior staff.
34.           Though overseas training of trainers has proceeded slowly, ABC did
conduct three courses in China in association with EDI in agricultural product
marketing (1987), Agribusiness Management (1988) and Project Monitoring and
Evaluation (1989) in which about 100 staff and teachers participated. Following
these three main courses, some 75 courses (with 3500 participants) were held in
different regional schools for local staff and teachers. The Tianjin Institute also
issued a text book in Chinese on agricultural project analysis which is being used
in secondary vocational and staff training schools. In April 1990, another training
course in project monitoring was held in China with EDI participation. Among other
initiatives, ABC issued in 1988, a manual on pasture and sheep development (prepared
with bilateral assistance from New Zealand) and a manual on agroprocessing (with
bilateral assistance from Sweden) is under preparation. A manual on Monitoring and
Evaluation prepared by ABC with assistance from the Bank Group staff is being tested
in the field and would be issued shortly.
35.           The training of trainers is critical to accelerate the training of a
large number of ABC's senior and middle level staff and would be continued under the
proposed project. Allowing for the unused funds under Rural Credit III project, the
proposed project would provide an additional amount of $50,000 for trainers training
and equipment and purchase of training material, books and equipment for the three
colleges, and for financing visits of overseas trainers to China. It is expected
that during 1991-92, a total of six courses would be held in China in project
appraisal, project monitoring and agroindustry financing in China (Annex 9, Table 1,
item 6)
36.           Training of project-related staff and credit staff in other
provinces. Besides strengthening ABC's in-house training capabilities through
trainers' training program, the project would support special courses in project
appraisal, project management and monitoring, and agroindustry financing for
projectlcredit staff at the headquarters (from all units concerned with medium-and
long-term credit); in project provinces and Beijing; and in other provinces not so
far covered by any of the Bank Group/IFAD/ADB projects (item 7 of Annex 9, Table 1).
ABC would also send key officials abroad for a well structured trainirg program in
project appraisal and management techniques and for exposure to project
implementation experience in other countries.
37            At negotiations, the approach to ABC's staff training (including
trainers' training) program as above was agreed to with ABC and the government. The
training program for CY1991 would be firmed up by January 1991 and ABC would send
subsequent annual training programs for the Bank Group's prior concurrence by about
end September of the preceding year.
6/ For details of ABC's staff training arrangements, see Annex 10 of Staff
Appraisal Report for Third Rural Credit Project Report No. 6903-CHA dated
December 28, 1987.

VI. OFFICE TECHNOLOGY AND EQUIPMENT
38.            Purchase of office technology equipment. ABC lacks basic facilities
such as telex and facsimile machines, copiers. electric typewriters, personal
computers, telephones etc at the head office and in provincial branches covered by
the proposed project. The project would finance this equipment subject to the Bank
Group's procurement guidelines and with prior approval of the Bank Group to the
overall procurement plan indicating categoriei of items to be procured, timing of
procurement, approximate costs and the list of offices which would receive the
equipment (Annex 9, Table 1, items 9 and 10).

Annex 11
CHINA
FOURTH RURAL CREDIT PROJECT
Onlending Terms and Conditions and Criteria for Sublan Approval
A. Onlending Terms: Government to ABC
1.    Loan period.  15 years including 5 years grace;
2.    Interest rates. On Bank Group funds onlent in local currency, government to
charge interest at a nonsubsidized rate which shall be based on ABC's weighted
average interest cost of long term domestic funds calculated in accordance with
criteria acceptable to the Bank Group (currently at about 7.5X-8Z p.a.). This rate
will be calculated annually and adjusted accordingly. On Bank Group funds onlent in
foreign currency at a rate not lower than the prevailing IBRD loan rate or such
other market-based rate acceptable to the Bank Group;
3.    Interest spread.  Government to ensure that ABC receives a spread of not less
than 2.5? (after payment of income and adjustment taxes to MOF) or such other spread
as may be agreed between the Government and the Bank Group, between the cost to ABC
of project funds (Bank Group funds and ABC's counterpart contribution) and the
project's onlending rates to subborrowers.
4.    Exchange risk to be borne by the Government except on proceeds of IDA
Credit/IBRD Loan onlent in foreign exchange, ABC to bear exchange risk; on subloans
disbursed by ABC in foreign exchange, ABC to pass on exchange risk to subborrowers;
5.    Commitment charge.  Any commitment fee charged by IDA and the commitment fee
on IBRD Loan will be passed on by the government to ABC.
B.    Onlending Terms: ABC to Subborrowers
1.    Eligible Subprojects.  Subprojects in agriculture including crops, livestock,
aquaculture and agroprocessing which would contibute to increased production and
productivity will be financed by the project. ABC will finance only sound
investments which are considered to be technically feasible, financially viable and
economically justified. Only subprojects which are estimated to yield financial and
economic rates of return of at least 15? determined in accordance with
internationally accepted techniques of project appraisal (as outlined in the ABC
project appraisal manual prepared with Bank Group assistance) will be financed by
the project. In appraising subprojects, ABC will pay particular attention to:
choice of technology, scale of development proposed, market analysis, availability
of inputs (raw materials, energy, packing materia'ls in the case of agro-processing
subprojects), financial condition and management capability of subborrowers, and
environmental aspects.
2.    Project Beneficiaries.  ABC subborrowers will include individuals, individual
and specialized households, collectives, cooperatives, township and village



120
enterprises, state farms, enterprises and intermediary companies set up by the state
farms, local government bureaus and supply and marketing cooperatives.
Subborrowers' credit eligibility will be determined by ABC's established criteria,
including ability to contribute a specified down payment toward the project cost,
pay interest on subloans during gestation periods, and possession of a satisfactory
credit rating.
3.    Enterprise Appraisal.  ABC will carry out enterprise appraisals of borrowing
state farms and intermediary companies (usually for retailing credit to households
and collectives and providing support services including extension, input supply andj
processing and marketing of output) to ensure that these entities are financially
viable, are managed efficiently, and have the capability to handle the project
funds. Enterprise appraisals would follow the outline indicated in the Attachment.
4.    Technical Feasibility.  ABC's Project Management Committees and the project
offices will be primarily responsible to verify whether technical feasibility
studies of subprojects have been properly carried out and that technology choices
are appropriate. ABC Head Office will use the Technical Expert Group to oversee
branches' compliance with sound technical criteria in subproject appraisal and
implementation (SAR, paras. 5.5-5.6). Technical and other standards and conditions
which the Bank Group and ABC will agree upon through project documentation and
during negotiations and supervision will be observed.
5.          TEG will assist ABC's operational staff in the review and supervision of
ABC's agricultural Lie*nding operations, interact with national line ministries and
provincial bureaus on sectoral development policies and programs, liaise with IDA
missions during supervision, and participate in ABC Head Office or provincial
supervision. All major slaughterhouse proposals including designs and layouts will
be subject to a review by an expert group set up by TEG.
6.          Technical feasibility studies on subprojects will be carried out by
experienced staff of government technical bureaus, design institutes, CAS and CAAS
institutes, universities and engineering schools as the Project Management
Committees and ABC project offices may consider appropriate. ABC may also hire, if
necessary, independent consultants to appraise subprojects or provide a second
opinion on the feasibility reports prepared by local agencies.
7.    Subloan approval limits.  Generally, subloans up to Y200,000 would be approved
at the county subbranch, subloans above Y200,000 to 500,000 at the prefecture level,
above Y500,000 to Y10,000,000 at the provincial level, and subloans above
Y10,000,000 at ABC Head Office. Subprojects involving introduction of new
technologies or where lower level branches and government bureaus are not familiar
with technologies involved, will be reviewed by higher level branches irrespective
of the subloan amounts.
8.    Subloans above free limit. Subloans in excess of US$3 million equivalent would
require prior approval of the Bank Group.
9.    Interest rates.  Subborrowers will pay interest on subloans at rates which
shall not be lower than ABC's prevailing rates for similar subloans for similar
purposes, or such other annual rate as shall be positive in real terms and which
shall permit ABC to retain an interest spread of at least 2.5Z (after payment of



121
income and adjustment taxes to MOF), or such other spread as may be agreed between
the government and the Bank Group. ABC's onlending rates would be reviewed with the
Bank Group from time to time (at least annually) and would be adjusted as needed, in
light of ABC's cost of project funds, spreads and profitability and taking into
account inflation in order to ensure that interest rates to final beneficiaries are
positive in real terms. Within this framework, ABC would automatically adjust
interest rates to final beneficiaries to ensure that these are positive in real
terms, without waiting for the adjustment of onlending rates between MOF and ABC.
10.   Capitalization of Interest during grace periods.  Since ABC's accounting
procedures do not admit capitalization of interest fallen due during the grace
periods for repayment of subloans, ABC would ensure that subborrowers have the
capability to pay the interest amount until the net revenue from the proposed
subproject is adequate to pay the interest on loan. If necessary for successful
completion of a subproject, ABC will allow capitalization of interest during the
grace periods.
11.   Subborrower contribution.  Subborrower contribution (including own labor and
other contributions in cash or kind) would not be less than 302 of the total project
cost (exclusive of interest due during grace period of subloans).
12.   Subloan maturities.  Subloan maturities will be determined on the basis of an
estimated cash flow of subprojects financed, generally within 5 years for high value
seasonal crops, 7 years for livestock and fisheries, 10 years for tree crops and 5-
10 years for agroprocessing, inclusive of appropriate grace periods. Subloan
periods will not generally exceed 12 years but no more than 15. In determining
subloan maturities, due regard will be given to the repaying capacity of
subborrowers' including their obligation to pay interest on subloans during the
grace period of loans and the amount that could be retained by subborrowers for
subsistence. Also, in subloan agreements, ABC will have the right to reduce the
subloan maturities fixed at the time of the disbursement if the nominal net revenue
from the subproject financed, can bear the additional debt service charges.
13.   Collateral.  Subloans will be secured and guranteed consistent with current
banking practices and ABC regulations.
14.   Supervision.  ABC branches will supervise the use of subloans and their
collection on due dates and introduce the loan portfolio monitoring system recently
introduced by ABC on a pilot basis, to project financed subloans.
15.   Reporting.  ABC branches will require subborrowers to submit reports and
information on performance of subprojects financed which ABC may reasonably request.
C. Criteria for RCC Access to Project funds
In most situations, ABC would use rural credit cooperatives (RCCs) and supply
and marketlng cooperatives (SHCs) on an agency basis for subloan collections or
provision of marketing services. Since SMCs are mainly marketing institutions,
provide only short-term advances to farmers against commodity purchase contracts,
and have no credit staff to appraise and supervise loans, ABC would not use SMCs for
onlending of project funds. If ABC decides to use RCCs for onlending of project
funds, it would select individual RCCs for participation in the project on the



122
following criteria;
1.    Financial condition.  A participating RCC shall have a satisfactory financial
condition as determined by recent ABC/SAA audit;
2.    Equity.  A participating RCC's own funds (stocks, membership contributions,
balance of undistributed profits and free reserves) shall not be less than 62 of the
total loans outstanding as of December 31 of each year;
3.    Past-dues.  A participating RCC's past-due loans shall not exceed 202 of the
total loans outstanding, and past-due loans in arrears for more than 3 years (which
are not covered by bad debts reserves), shall not exceed 5? of the outstanding loans
as of December 31 of each year;
4.    Liquidity.  A participating RCC shall have complied with minimum reserve
requirements (generally RCC redeposit of 20-302 of its total deposits, with ABC) and
most recent annual operations shall be in profit; and
S.    Trained staff. A participating RCC shall have at least one credit officer
trained in the project appraisal courses offered by ABC;
6.    Onlending Terms: RCC to subborrowers.  Onlending terms between RCC to
subborrwers will be similar to those for ABC to subborrowers (section B above)
except that interest rates to be charged to subborrowers would not be lower than
RCCs' prevailing rates for similar subloans for similar purposes or such other
annual interest rate as shall be positive in real terms, and which shall permit
RCCs' to retain a minimum spread of 2.5Z (after payment of income and adjustment
taxes) which shall be shared between ABC and RCC according to mutual agreement;
7.    Onlending Terms: ABC to RCCs. Interest on ABC loans to RCCs will be set at a
rate based on ABC's prevailing rates for similar loans to RCCs (using criteria in
para. A-2 above), and RCCs would provide from their own funds 20? of the subproject
cost; and
8.    Repayment period.  ABC will prescribe the same repayme4nt schedule for its own
loans to RCCs as fixed by a RCC to a subborrower, with a suitable grace period;
D.    General.
1.    ABC Head Office and branches and participating RCCs and SMCs will maintain
separate accounts for project funds;
2.    ABC will allow rescheduling of project-assisted subloans and interest in
accordance with ABC's existing guidelines and report the status to the Bank Group
annuslly, along with a table showing a summary of subloans and interest fallen due,
subloans collected, the amount of past due loans and interest, age-wise
classification of past due loans and interest, and the amount of subloans and
interest rescheduled, in a format acceptable to the Bank Group.
3.    The lending terms and conditions listed in sections A, B and C above may be
revised from time to time as agreed between the Bank Group, the government and ABC.

Annex 11
Attachment 1
CHINA
FOURTH RURAL CREDIT PROJECT
Enterprise Appraisal Outline
A.    For State Farms.
1.    Status of Reform.   ABC's appraisal of enterprises will ascertain whether the
state farm has decentralized its operations and, if so, the nature and extent of
such decentralization outlining the new relationship between the state farm's parent
body vis-a vis individual households, collectives within the state farm and the
enterprises sponsored by it;
2.    Sources and Uses of Funds.   The enterprise appraisal will assess the parent
state farm's main sources and uses of funds for the preceding three years indicating
whether the state farm is in surplus or deficit, and the extent of payments made
to/or subsidies received from the local state farm bureaus;
3.    Financial Condition of Enterprises.  The enterprise appraisal will comment
upon the financial condition of individual enterprises sponsored by the state firm
indicating whether they are in profit or loss for the past several years and their
aggregate liabilities to the state farm's parent body, financial projections for the
next 3 to 5 years, and whether the enterprises guaranteeing the subloan are
financially sound;
4.    Management.  The appraisal will assess the quality of the state farm's
management based on the past experience with ABC subloans and assess its capability
to provide efficient management for the proposed projects.
B.    For Enterprises
1.     Nature of Organization.  The appraisal will indicate the nature of the
subborrower's organization (such as a cooperative or a collective or a company);
2.    Charter.  The appraisal will ascertain whether the borrowing enterprise is an
independent economic entity; whether it has a charter; whether the charter has been
registered with the appropriate government agency; whether the enterprise's
objectives and operational aspects such as ownership, management, amount of own
funds (equity), sponsoring bureau's or agency's financial and management
responsibility, and arrangements for distribution of profits and sharing of losses,
have been defined in the charter. The enterprise appraisal will also comment upon
whether households for whose benefit the enterprise is being set up would have a
share in the ownership, management and profits of the company and whether the
proposed contractual arrangements between the enterprises and participating



124
households are fair and equitable? If the enterprise (or a government-owned company)
is dissolved by any administrative decisions, the enterprise appraisal will assess
what will be the alternate arrangements to take over the functions of the borrowing
company and honor its liabilities to ABC? Does ABC enjoy a right to ask for a
change in the enterprise's management?
3.    Financial Condition.  The appraisal will analyze the enterprise's past three
years' operations including adequacy of owned funds, debt-equity ratio, performance
on repayment of past subloans, financial projections for the next 3-5 years, and
ability of the enterprise to provide self contribution for the proposed investment.
If self-contribution to the proposed investment is being made from the borrowed
funds, the appraisal will analyse the terms and conditions of such loans and ability
of the enterprise to repay such loans, and whether the repayment of ABC loans will
receive priority over other borrowings, and can ABC convert its loan into equity and
take over the management of the enterprise, if this course is appropriate.
4.    Guarantor.  The enterprise appraisal will assess the credit-worthiness of the
guarantor and ascertain whether the local government (finance bureau) or any other
government agency is responsible for repayment of the loan and whether the financial
condition of the guarantor or the concerned government agency is satisfactory. The
appraisal will also ascertain ABC's past experience with the concerned local
government's guarantees to determine whether these are dependable.
5.    Manager.  The enterprise appraisal will ascertain the professional background
and experience of the enterprise's manager; whether the manager can be removed if
not found suitable and if so, how and by whom; has ABC a right to suggest a change
in the enterprise management; is the borrowing enterprise obligated by an agreement
to honor ABC's suggestion?
6.    Staffing.  The enterprise appraisal will comment upon how the borrowing
enterprise's staff will be recruited; whether the qualifying criteria for the staff
are adequate to ensure smooth functioning of the enterprise; and whether unsuitable
staff can be removed and if so, how.
7.    Training.  The enterprise appraisal will comment 1pon whether the arrangements
for training of the manager and staff are satisfactory and whether training activity
has been included in the proposed subproject?
8.    External Audit and Financial Reporting.  The enterprise appraisal will review
whether arrangements for audit of and financial reporting on the enterprise are
satisfactory and whether ABC will get the audit reports regularly.
9.    General.  Participating ABC branches will prepare a report covering the above
aspects on all enterprises borrowing under the project as a basis for the subloan
decision.

Annex 14
CHINA
FOURTH RURAL CREDIT PROJECT
Related Documents and Data in the Project File
A. Project Preparation Reports and related documents
1.    Subproject feasibility studies and appraisal reports (in Chinese) prepared by
ABC and local governments in the following provinces: Inner Mongolia, Jilin,
Liaoning, Shaanxi, Jiangxi, Guangdong, and Beijing municipality.
2.    Executive Project Summary dated October 30, 1989
3.    Identification, Preparation, Preappraisal and Appraisal Missions' BTOs
and Aide Memoires
B.    Working Papers by Consultants on Preparation, Preappraisal and Appraisal
Missions
1.    Review of Livestock proposals in Inner Mongolia, Jilin and Beijing by FAO/CP
Preparation Mission.
2.    Review of Grassland-based Livestock proposals by D. Sheehy.
3.    Review of Livestock proposals in Inner Mongolia, Shaanxi, Jilin, Liaoning and
Beijing by P. Gilchrist.
4.    Review of Gong Qing State Farm's Duck production and processing subprojects
by J. Hald.
5.    Review of Apple planting and rehabilitation proposals in Shaanxi, Liaoning and
Beijing by W. Baier and E. Bayer.
6.    Review of Aquaculture component by R. Zweig.
7.    Appraisal of Tea Rehabilitation component in Jiangxi by R. K. Nathaniel.
8.    Review of Tree and annual crops proposals by Paul Liou.
9.    Financial Appraisal of ABC operations in Jiangxi and Liaoning by Sema Group of
France.
10.   Review of agroprocessing subprojects by C. Cronberg.
11.   Financial and Economic Rates of Return.
